  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_f7a27457-a305-42ac-bf7c-3f127002331b'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<div id="review_254961433" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/254961433" />
    <a title="Branimir" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4442035-branimir"><img alt="Branimir" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542881127p2/4442035.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/254961433?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Branimir" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Branimir" href="/user/show/4442035-branimir">Branimir</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9783932068027" href="/book/show/5972256-tvr-ava">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4442035-branimir?shelf=bosnia">bosnia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4442035-branimir?shelf=must-read-again">must-read-again</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4442035-branimir?shelf=ex-yugoslavia">ex-yugoslavia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer254961433" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12856641657494790654">I read this book long ago, back in high school. It left a really deep mark on me, it changed the way I, a young teen, viewed the world - exactly the thing a good book is supposed to do. The author, Meša Selimović, along with Ivo Andrić, the famed Nobel Prize Winner, is one of the best 20th-century Bosnian writers. In this work he succeeded in capturing the "soul" of ordinary Bosnian folk.<br/><br/>Selimović uses Ahmet Šabo, the novel's protagonist, to express himself about general things in life:<br/><br/><blockquote>Experienc</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText12856641657494790654" style="display:none">I read this book long ago, back in high school. It left a really deep mark on me, it changed the way I, a young teen, viewed the world - exactly the thing a good book is supposed to do. The author, Meša Selimović, along with Ivo Andrić, the famed Nobel Prize Winner, is one of the best 20th-century Bosnian writers. In this work he succeeded in capturing the "soul" of ordinary Bosnian folk.<br /><br />Selimović uses Ahmet Šabo, the novel's protagonist, to express himself about general things in life:<br /><br /><blockquote>Experience had taught me that what you can’t explain to yourself is better told to another. You can deceive yourself with just one part of the picture that happens to impose itself with a feeling difficult to express, since it hides in the face of the pain of comprehension and flies into the mists, into the intoxication that seeks no meaning. For the other, exact speech is essential, and this forces you to seek it, to feel its presence somewhere within you, and to grasp it, it or its shadow, so as to recognize it in another’s face, in another’s glance, as he begins to comprehend it. The listener is the midwife in the difficult birth of the word. Or, still more important—if he desires to understand.</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="12856641657494790654" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_15820317" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/15820317" />
    <a title="Ed" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/922735-ed"><img alt="Ed" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1313272000p2/922735.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/15820317?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ed" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ed" href="/user/show/922735-ed">Ed</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/922735-ed?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/922735-ed?shelf=central-and-eastern-europe">central-and-eastern-europe</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer15820317" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17282736197954610459">Why I feel compelled to include in all my 'reviews' some background on why I read a book, who recommended/gave it to me, etc: I write these primarily as an exercise/diary for my own benefit and it's nice for me to keep track of this.<br/><br/>A couple days before I left for Europe, an old friend from high school with whom I have had only spotty contact for the last decade--Kemal--showed up at an ad hoc see-Ed-off gathering at Liquor Lyle's in Minneapolis. Kemal is Bosnian, he turned up in Minneapolis and </span>
  <span id="freeText17282736197954610459" style="display:none">Why I feel compelled to include in all my 'reviews' some background on why I read a book, who recommended/gave it to me, etc: I write these primarily as an exercise/diary for my own benefit and it's nice for me to keep track of this.<br /><br />A couple days before I left for Europe, an old friend from high school with whom I have had only spotty contact for the last decade--Kemal--showed up at an ad hoc see-Ed-off gathering at Liquor Lyle's in Minneapolis. Kemal is Bosnian, he turned up in Minneapolis and at South High in the mid nineties when all that shit was going down. I've never spoken with him really about his life before fleeing Bosnia, and the only granule of information I have about it is the rumor that he lived hiding in a garage with his family for some incredibly unthinkably long time before finally escaping. I was given to assume that he didn't talk much about Bosnia, didn't think much about it, was just moving on...<br /><br />That's why I was surprised at how animated he became when at Lyle's I mentioned, for some reason, having read Ivo Andric's Nobel-Prize-winning Bridge on the Drina. He adores Bosnian literature, it turns out, and had to turn me onto the 'really good stuff' because Bridge pales in comparison. Not surprisingly, English translations of the 'really good stuff' have been rather difficult to find in Switzerland. But Janie got me The Fortress for my birthday!<br /><br />Happy birthday Ed, I know this is your favorite: dark tales of misery, poverty, violence and alienation in a forgotten corner of the world. I'm not being sarcastic; for some reason I really do love this stuff.<br /><br />With all that as a preface, clearly I was prepared to really love this book. Right up my alley, and with a recommendation from a source with above-average reliability.<br /><br />And it WAS really good. There are moments of incredibly transcendent writing in The Fortress, it has surprisingly optimistic overtones and is moralistic without being preachy. I especially appreciate how conflicted the narrator is about how to interpret people and situations he encounters, and how his perspectives on human behavior and the thrust of life change so often but so naturally and understandably over the year or so that the book appears to cover.<br /><br />But I found it very difficult to follow the thread of the story. I was more than halfway through the book before I realized that characters who had initially played incidental roles were suddenly at the center of the plot and I should have gotten to know them better when they were introduced. Where was this guy from again? How is he related to this other guy? Who's got a grudge against whom and why? Paging back I found that playing catch-up was not possible. There is simply a lot of information left out. It is possible that I could blame the translation; the translator's note at the beginning mentioned that in order to make the book readable in English the Serbo-Croatian had to be simplified (in other words, presumably, butchered).<br /><br />Still, this and other jarringly non-linear aspects of the story are not without their benefit; it does give the feeling overall of a complex social atmosphere in 19th century Sarajevo which is clearly as difficult to navigate for the narrator as it is for the reader. And, as I mentioned before, it contributes to the credibility of the narrator's own shifting perspectives. But unfortunately, even though The Fortress is full of messages of greater value, the thought I was left with on the last page was, 'shit, I'm gonna have to read this again.' If I were asked what happened in the book, I guarantee I would not be able to explain it. So much seemed so unconnected to so much else, and the shifting ways characters behaved towards one another sometimes appeared almost random, to the point that it was not possible to trust your own impressions of who anybody is or just what the eff is going on. My generous side continues to suggest to me that this is probably the point...but I don't know. Have to give it another shot.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17282736197954610459" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_184031912" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/184031912" />
    <a title="Bojan Tunguz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5226097-bojan-tunguz"><img alt="Bojan Tunguz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1302009626p2/5226097.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/184031912?book_show_action=true">Jul 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bojan Tunguz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bojan Tunguz" href="/user/show/5226097-bojan-tunguz">Bojan Tunguz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer184031912" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11894057965194924619">Ahmet Shabo is a young man from 18th century Ottoman Bosnia, who returns to his native Sarajevo after experiencing all the horrors of war during battles in distant Russia. The war has a major psychological effect on him, and he seems unable or unwilling to rejoin the society. A kind friend offers him a decent job with which he'll be able to support himself and perhaps even advance in social circles. Things go wrong for Ahmet, however, after a party that he gets invited to thrown by some very imp</span>
  <span id="freeText11894057965194924619" style="display:none">Ahmet Shabo is a young man from 18th century Ottoman Bosnia, who returns to his native Sarajevo after experiencing all the horrors of war during battles in distant Russia. The war has a major psychological effect on him, and he seems unable or unwilling to rejoin the society. A kind friend offers him a decent job with which he'll be able to support himself and perhaps even advance in social circles. Things go wrong for Ahmet, however, after a party that he gets invited to thrown by some very important city officials. His struggles to reclaim a place in the World become the main focal point of the book from then on. <br /><br />Like in his more famous novel "The Death and the Dervish," Selimovi'c manages to embed the personal struggles of one man under a totalitarian communist regime into a much more distant past and an equally oppressive medieval Ottoman rule. One can imagine that writing under the keen watchful eye of a communist state made Selimovi' resort to this tactic. Selimovi'c is also an exceptional stylist. You can find remarkable and insightful sentences on almost every page of the book. Also, almost all of the dialogues have a deep philosophical undertone to them. Selimovic''s insights into human psyche are uncanny, and the lessons that he draws from them are timeless. Perhaps the most famous of his insights is the claim, put into the mouth of one of the protagonists, that there are three major vices that we are tempted towards: alcohol, gambling and power. While we can overcome the first two, the last one is unconquerable. <br /><br />The main struggle that Ahmet is engaged in is not with his opponents who make his life extremely difficult. It is rather an internal struggle between accepting one of the two opposing worldviews: a fatalist one where the life's events are so far outside one's control that is meaningless to take any personal initiative, and a much more individualist worldview that affirms the value of an individual and supports the notion that our individual strivings have a meaning and a positive effect on our lives. <br /><br />The fortress from the title is the motif that acquires many different meanings throughout the book. It is a physical place that is instrumental to the plot, but it also represents several different life circumstances and states of mind. Selimovi' adroitly exploits all of these multiple meanings, and effortlessly shuffles between them without the danger of overusing the metaphor. <br /><br />This is one of two Selimovic''s great works and in every respect as good as "The Death and the Dervish." It needs to be read by anyone who wants to get a better understanding of the life, relationships, and the historical circumstances that have shaped Bosnia through the centuries. The book also cements Selimovi''s place as one of the great World writers of the twentieth century.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11894057965194924619" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2053092199?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Farebrother" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Farebrother" href="/user/show/68609816-john-farebrother">John Farebrother</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9788674704073" href="/book/show/35619415-tvr-ava">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2053092199" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10047351034972607034">Another of Selimović's masterpieces. In many ways it is a variation on the theme of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8487092.Dervi__i_smrt' title='Derviš i smrt by Meša Selimović' rel='nofollow noopener'>Derviš i smrt</a>, although its reputation as a great book in its own right is fully justified. Like its predecessor, it is set in medieval Bosnia, and its description of the intrigues and injustices of Ottoman rule are an allegory for Tito's Yugoslavia (although the book was never banned). The pure cynicism, unbridled greed and dark ambition of public servants in a totalitarian state is mercilessly exposed:<br/><br/>“If this </span>
  <span id="freeText10047351034972607034" style="display:none">Another of Selimović's masterpieces. In many ways it is a variation on the theme of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8487092.Dervi__i_smrt" title="Derviš i smrt by Meša Selimović" rel="nofollow noopener">Derviš i smrt</a>, although its reputation as a great book in its own right is fully justified. Like its predecessor, it is set in medieval Bosnia, and its description of the intrigues and injustices of Ottoman rule are an allegory for Tito's Yugoslavia (although the book was never banned). The pure cynicism, unbridled greed and dark ambition of public servants in a totalitarian state is mercilessly exposed:<br /><br />“If this is how they avenge themselves against sparrows, what would they do to a sparrowhawk? […] Why do they need this revenge? To frighten me? To frighten others by my example? To exult over the weak? To ban thought? To ban speech? […] They humiliated me by someone else’s hand, for everything they have willing perpetrators, eternal servants, without conscience and without reason, as they are, the only difference being that they have the power of judgment and authority over others.”<br /><br />It is also an authentic portrayal of medieval Bosnia, sandwiched between the political power of Istanbul and the economic might of Venice, with the rival spheres of influence of Austria-Hungary and Russia never far away. But above all, it is the story of an ordinary demobbed soldier trying to find his way in civvie street.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10047351034972607034" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1010052883" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1010052883" />
    <a title="Natasha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30539446-natasha"><img alt="Natasha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1468853832p2/30539446.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1010052883?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Natasha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Natasha" href="/user/show/30539446-natasha">Natasha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30539446-natasha?shelf=touching-overwhelming">touching-overwhelming</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30539446-natasha?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30539446-natasha?shelf=descriptive">descriptive</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1010052883" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17879860335351272229"> native of the former Yugoslavia, Selimovic is of Muslim descent and writes in Serbo-Croatian. Delving deep into the Bosnian past in this dense historical novel, he charts the 18th-century adventures of narrator Ahmet Shabo, who returns with a heavy heart to Sarajevo from the battle of Chocim, in Russia. Discovering that almost all of his family has died of the plague, Ahmet first works for Mula Ibrahim, a clerk he rescued in battle. The disaffected ex-soldier loses the job when he insults a pow</span>
  <span id="freeText17879860335351272229" style="display:none"> native of the former Yugoslavia, Selimovic is of Muslim descent and writes in Serbo-Croatian. Delving deep into the Bosnian past in this dense historical novel, he charts the 18th-century adventures of narrator Ahmet Shabo, who returns with a heavy heart to Sarajevo from the battle of Chocim, in Russia. Discovering that almost all of his family has died of the plague, Ahmet first works for Mula Ibrahim, a clerk he rescued in battle. The disaffected ex-soldier loses the job when he insults a powerful man at a party, but luckily, he has married a Christian orphan who is as hardworking as she is beautiful. While she makes money, Ahmet idles around Sarajevo and ponders his own fate and that of his countrymen. Ahmet's perspective gives Selimovic a perfect opportunity to describe the Ottoman-ruled Sarajevo of 300 hundred years ago, riven then, as it is today, with hidden feuds and power politics. One of Ahmet's comrades from Chocim, student Ramiz, stirs up trouble by speaking out in the mosque against the wealthy and powerful. For this he is imprisoned in the formidable fortress of the title. Ahmet goes to a rich man, Shehaga, to plead his friend's case, and for reasons of his own, Shehaga and his dashing steward, Osman Vuk, arrange a raid upon the prison, freeing Ramiz. Ahmet is then trapped in a conspiracy of silence with Osman and Shehaga, while the serder-Avdaga, a self-appointed law enforcer and scourge of God, sniffs out the truth. Although there are some powerful scenes, American readers may not appreciate Selimovic's glacial pace. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17879860335351272229" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5494824528142622504">The summary of this book in no way describes how beautiful it is. It's many stories, like fables, woven into a greater story Instead of morals, however, the stories illustrate gentle Koranic philosophy.<br/>The characters are vibrant, likeable, funny, tender.<br/>One of my favourite parts:<br/><br/>"Man is surrounded my fear as by flame, drowned in it, as in water.   <span id="freeText5494824528142622504" style="display:none">The summary of this book in no way describes how beautiful it is. It's many stories, like fables, woven into a greater story Instead of morals, however, the stories illustrate gentle Koranic philosophy.<br />The characters are vibrant, likeable, funny, tender.<br />One of my favourite parts:<br /><br />"Man is surrounded my fear as by flame, drowned in it, as in water. He fears fate, the morrow, the law, a stronger man; and he isn't what he wants to be but what he has to be. He fawns before fate, prays to the morrow, blindly follows the law, smiles humbly at the man in power whom he hates, reconciled to being a monstrous creation made up of fear and obedience.<br />If man was sometimes sad, it was because he recalled himself as he was in his dreams, as if he could have been, were he not as he was. As if the world were not what it is.<br />I wouldn't have it like that!"</span>

<div id="review_3123008192" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3123008192" />
    <a title="Paul" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3643002-paul"><img alt="Paul" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1411919268p2/3643002.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3123008192?book_show_action=true">Jan 04, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/3643002-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3643002-paul?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3643002-paul?shelf=period-drama">period-drama</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3123008192" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6912303731621517603">In Meša Selimović's <i>The Fortress</i>, Ahmet Shabo, a Bosnian Muslim, returns home to 18th century Sarajevo after fighting for the Ottoman Empire in Russia. The sole survivor of his unit, he discovers that his family has died of disease while he was away. Alone, but also newly contemplative in the wake of his wartime experiences, Shabo is content to wander and think and appreciate the natural world around him, but the world has other plans. A man whose life he saved gives him a job, he finds love wit</span>
  <span id="freeText6912303731621517603" style="display:none">In Meša Selimović's <i>The Fortress</i>, Ahmet Shabo, a Bosnian Muslim, returns home to 18th century Sarajevo after fighting for the Ottoman Empire in Russia. The sole survivor of his unit, he discovers that his family has died of disease while he was away. Alone, but also newly contemplative in the wake of his wartime experiences, Shabo is content to wander and think and appreciate the natural world around him, but the world has other plans. A man whose life he saved gives him a job, he finds love with a young Christian woman, and he is soon both married and gainfully employed.<br /><br />When he is invited to a dinner by the city's elite in honor of his military service, however, Shabo speaks openly about what he sees as the evils of Bosnian society - with the wealthy and powerful unaccountable, and the poor and disenfranchised suffering under their boot heel. He is badly beaten for his impertinence, loses his position as a scribe, and is relegated to the fringes of Sarajevo society - unable to secure work or assistance. <br /><br />It is then that he encounters Ramiz, a young firebrand who preaches at the mosque that the common folk must cast off their chains, and it is then that he is asked to spy on Ramiz by the people who have made him an outcast. What follows are events and encounters that force Shabo to adapt to changing circumstances while fighting desperately to maintain his integrity and sense of self. He meets rogues, murderers, merchants, bandits, and a man who even the religious and political leadership of Sarajevo fear, and he becomes embroiled in a scheme that will shake the city elders to their bones.<br /><br />Simply, but beautifully written, <i>The Fortress</i> is populated with deceptively complex characters, and there is a palpable sense of the time and place in the story. The plot is both meandering and straightforward, occurring almost organically, as events in real life tend to do, and Shabo is both admirable and believable as a protagonist. Where the books stands out, however, is in his internal dialogues, as he fights to maintain his principles in a world that has little use for them, and in the conversations he has with other people who have decidedly different approaches to life. <br /><br />There are insights wonderfully and uniquely expressed on most pages, and while this is a novel that moves at a steady pace, the reader is rewarded for stopping, re-reading, and thinking about what the author has written and his characters have to say.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6912303731621517603" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2671811898" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2671811898" />
    <a title="Rasmiya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/92074226-rasmiya"><img alt="Rasmiya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1549728538p2/92074226.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2671811898?book_show_action=true">Jan 18, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rasmiya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rasmiya" href="/user/show/92074226-rasmiya">Rasmiya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2671811898" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2079846305870020772">Truly, another masterpiece by Meša Selimović! His 'Death and the Dervish' had a strong impression on me and the author managed to achieve the same effect with 'The Fortress'. And he did that both through the story line, and the way he usually narrates his stories using black humour and diving unimaginably deep below the surface, when describing thoughts, feelings and emotions. Although the story is set in Bosnia in 1700s, the ugly face of power and the reactions of those affected, meaningless wa</span>
  <span id="freeText2079846305870020772" style="display:none">Truly, another masterpiece by Meša Selimović! His 'Death and the Dervish' had a strong impression on me and the author managed to achieve the same effect with 'The Fortress'. And he did that both through the story line, and the way he usually narrates his stories using black humour and diving unimaginably deep below the surface, when describing thoughts, feelings and emotions. Although the story is set in Bosnia in 1700s, the ugly face of power and the reactions of those affected, meaningless wars and people behaving in peacetime worse than during wars, the feeling of longing for a mother country regardless of other places offering a better destiny, etc., are as relevant today, as they were then. These could also resonate well with the reality of any country. When it comes to his way of narrating the story, the works of this author (incl. The Fortress) appeal to me for 3 reasons: first, he manages to skilfully pin down the swirl of thoughts that usually go through a very reflective and sensitive human mind; secondly, in describing feelings, the author is very good in finding associations, which make it impossible not to apprehend the experiences of his heroes; and thirdly, he has an awesome sense of humour, even when touching on difficult subjects. I also like him for the fact that his works always make the reader explore the meaning of the same words as you go through the book. For instance, in this novel, he often uses the word 'fortress' but refers to different things at different times: sometimes the implications are negative (when the reference is to the prison which restricts freedom and promises only death), sometimes extremely positive (when referring to the security offered by the loved ones), and this adds another layer of perspective. To sum up, this one is definitely worth the time reading and is definitely not for the idle minds. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2079846305870020772" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Sanja Kolar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/52248299-sanja-kolar"><img alt="Sanja Kolar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546685023p2/52248299.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1728886991?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sanja Kolar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sanja Kolar" href="/user/show/52248299-sanja-kolar">Sanja Kolar</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1728886991" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7492937821244326175">I've just finished reading this book for the first time and the least I can say is that I am impressed.<br/><br/>"The Fortress" is a novel written in a beautiful, but not purple prose-ish language, packed with deep philosophical questions and answers, which give us an insight into the writer's own inner dialogues, which are open for readers' further thinking. Also, the problems that the characters in the novel face can easily be applied both to when Meša Selimović lived, and to the present time. The gover</span>
  <span id="freeText7492937821244326175" style="display:none">I've just finished reading this book for the first time and the least I can say is that I am impressed.<br /><br />"The Fortress" is a novel written in a beautiful, but not purple prose-ish language, packed with deep philosophical questions and answers, which give us an insight into the writer's own inner dialogues, which are open for readers' further thinking. Also, the problems that the characters in the novel face can easily be applied both to when Meša Selimović lived, and to the present time. The government hasn't changed much, only its brutality has waned a little. It is even true to the novel itself - there was a passage where Ahmet notices it too. Rule and power is a circle which cannot be unturned or a chain that's unlikely to be broken - just shaped a little here and there, to make it seem more 'modern' or 'better' than it was in some distant past, but still the same in its core. <br /><br />Everything about this book is excellent - the prose and the language, the characters and their choices and consequences, the plot and the path it went through.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7492937821244326175" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2147366330" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2147366330" />
    <a title="Catherine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12737918-catherine"><img alt="Catherine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1347377025p2/12737918.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2147366330?book_show_action=true">Oct 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Catherine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Catherine" href="/user/show/12737918-catherine">Catherine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789958290541" href="/book/show/36376426-tvr-ava">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12737918-catherine?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2147366330" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2708624204587863911">Was there ever a time in human history when man didn't feel lonely and lost? Was there ever a place where he could feel he truly belonged? <br/><br/>Ahmet Šabo returns from war remembering why each of the soldiers from his unit joined the army and how they died. Already lost after experiencing horrors of war (and also horrors of what would today be called a war crime) he finds out upon his return that his entire family died while he was away. Ahmet falls in love and accepts a job offer from a man whose li</span>
  <span id="freeText2708624204587863911" style="display:none">Was there ever a time in human history when man didn't feel lonely and lost? Was there ever a place where he could feel he truly belonged? <br /><br />Ahmet Šabo returns from war remembering why each of the soldiers from his unit joined the army and how they died. Already lost after experiencing horrors of war (and also horrors of what would today be called a war crime) he finds out upon his return that his entire family died while he was away. Ahmet falls in love and accepts a job offer from a man whose life he saved. During formal dinner party for which his co-worker had to go to great lengths to get them invitations, he openly speaks his mind about society, government, and powers to be. This costs him his job and safety and turns him into almost pariah.<br /><br />The novel explores similar topics like The Dervish and The Death, but with more introspection. Titular fortress is built within main character who tries to find his place in the world which is corrupted and lives constantly in fear of government officials who climb social ladder by means of betrayal and favours. Even when given the option to ask for a favour from an influential official, he chooses to help another human being, for no other reason than plain and simple humanity. In the end, Ahmet remains sane and is able to continue not because of kindness he received, but because of small acts of kindness and respect he showed to other people, particularly those who would never be able to return the favour. He may not be back on his feet, he will never be successful or powerful, but he remains true to himself and his inner moral compass.<br /><br />Just like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8487092.Dervi__i_smrt" title="Derviš i smrt by Meša Selimović" rel="nofollow noopener">Derviš i smrt</a> this one will remain with me for a long time. Especially the description of a woman screaming alone in front of her house without a single neighbouring house anywhere in sight.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2708624204587863911" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('MtaIpbucDy3D3r+7KCcVelD4PMR/S8rncZVTfDMa42t/RUSowUckYhNY1QwjAbDYjQt/fiycsfsM55jDtw4PNg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('yeOxilhu48rhQL5gqJa3xYDXU2Ry3bLbjEqF1PvW3iaEcH2HIrXIhTHG1NejsBJnXSQQ3iEKycfxOE5rf8Iyew==')})
  }

</script>

Ćamil, a wealthy young man of Smyrna living in the last years of the Ottoman Empire, is fascinated by the story of Džem, ill-fated brother of the Sultan Bajazet, who ruled Turkey in the fifteenth century. Ćamil, in his isolation, comes to believe that he is Džem, and that he shares his evil destiny: he is born to be a victim of the State. Because of his stories about Džem’s ambitions to overthrow his brother, Ćamil is arrested under suspicion of plotting against the Sultan. He is taken to a prison in Istanbul, where he tells his story, to Petar, a monk.\n\nOut of these exotic materials, Andrić has constructed a book of great clarity, brevity and interest. No doubt it will be read by some as a political parable about the tyranny of the State, but also as a quite simply story about ill-fortune and human misunderstanding, fear and ignorance. Džem and Ćamil are doomed – and the certainty of their persecution is sometimes relieved, sometimes intensified by the stupidity and fright of the people who cross their ill-starred lives.\n\nConstruction takes up most of the book’s space: the central story of Džem as related by Ćamil lasts only a chapter or two. For the rest of the time the reader strips layer off layer, as one narrator passes him on the next. There is an interesting passage that helps to explain this method, at the moment when Ćamil starts narrating Džem’s story in the first person. “I” is a word, we are told, which fixes the position of the speaker in such a way that the exercise of will is no longer possible, and the speaker strength is exceeded – strength, presumably, to break out of the identification that all his past actions and thoughts force upon him when he uses the word. “I” is both a confession and an imprisonment. The fact that the novel passes the reader on from one narrator to the next rather suggests that the author is taking constant evasive action, lest he betray himself or his reader into the kind of “personal confession” which seals the fate of Ćamil. What exactly this game of form flirting with meaning signifies, must be left to the individual reader.\n\nThe movement is centripetal, towards Džem’s story, and then disperses. Details within the story are made to mimic this form. Thus when Peter receives the message telling him of his impending release:\n\n“Two younger prisoners...were chasing around using him as the centerpoint of ever narrowing circles. Annoyed, he tried to break away from these exuberant youths when one of them brushed against him and he felt a folded scrap of paper thrust into his hand. The youths continued their chase but now in widening circles...”\n\nThe reader is led on just such a chase in the course of the novel. The effect of this is to make the plot seem more like a poetic image than an ordinary plot: capable, therefore, of as many meanings as are the images of an allusive poem. Yet the language is simple and direct, not at all “poetic”. The characters are remarkable alive, even in conversation. Karađoz, the governor of the goal, is a spidery authoritarian, who loves to torment the charges he loves. The prisoners “complained about the way one complains about one’s life and curses one’s destiny...it would have been hard for them to imagine life without him”.\n\n“The Devil’s Yard” is justified, as all symbolic and figurative novels must be, by the extent to which it touches the emotions. It is extremely moving. Fear, horror, despair, amusement at times – all these indicate that the threat of the meaning has been recognized.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"8189054259669887849\" href=\"#\" //<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_1743453'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1743453.Gospo_ica?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Gospođica<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/2147.Ivo_Andri_?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Ivo Andrić<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.20 avg rating &mdash; 1,508 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1945\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer4141668451540532304\">Gospođica (The Woman from Sarajevo) by Andrić was originally published in 1945. and is one of the three novels that make up the \"Bosnian Trilogy\". The other two are Bosnian Chronicle and The Bridge on the Drina.\n\nThe novel is set in the cities of Sar<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText4141668451540532304\" style=\"display:none\">Gospođica (The Woman from Sarajevo) by Andrić was originally published in 1945. and is one of the three novels that make up the &quot;Bosnian Trilogy&quot;. The other two are Bosnian Chronicle and The Bridge on the Drina.\n\nThe novel is set in the cities of Sarajevo and Belgrade during the first three decades of this century. The places and time are not incidentally chosen. Ivo Andrić is from Bosnia and knows the people and their problems in this unique area. The theme and composition suggest a work of modern classicism. It is the tragic life of a woman disappointed in people and in the world she lives in. She is completely enslaved by money, in which she hopes to find security and revenge in a hateful and insecure world. At the same time she is literally and feverishly following her bankrupt father’s last plea, as she becomes not only thrifty but a real miser in a classical Gogolian style.\n\nThis novel is dominated by a single character, a spinster named Miss Raika Radaković who is dominated by a single passion, stinginess. The development of this passion is traced through the first third of this century, from the time Miss Raika’s dying father, a ruined merchant, solemnly enjoins his school-girl-daughter to guard with her life the little property he can leave her, until some thirty-five years later when she herself dies, a scrawny old woman, unloved and unlovable, but faithful to her trust. As a moneylender she parlays small insurance legacy after her father into a small fortune. But whatever her wealth at any time, she will spend none of it on herself. For this single-minded greed for money she puts everything else out of her life – love, friendship, concern for other people.\n \nRaika also speculates in currencies during the tumultuous years of the Austro-Hungarian Empire and the uncertain emergence of the new Yugoslav state. A good deal of the power of Andrić’s tale comes through the depiction of this seething historical background, in whose torrent the wretched Raika’s life is washed along like a piece of flotsam.\n\nThe novel is carefully thought out and fully imagined, with solid passages of description in the nineteenth-century manner and scrupulous attentions to the job of relating the individual life to the broader pattern of social and economical change. With brilliant economy Andrić sketches the stages through which Miss Raika hoards and increases her inheritance: Sarajevo in the era of provincial usury, the economic upheavals and disintegration of the First World War, staid Belgrade headily embarking on the jazz age when the war is over, the pinched depression years during which Miss Raika dies. The requisite drama – the testing of the ruling passion – is present in the form of a young man whose physical resemblance to a beloved uncle long dead touches Miss Raika’s heart enough to make her swerve, though only momentarily, from her objective of never parting with her money.\n\n The chronicle is Andrić’s favorite form, and this novel is no exception, a highly poetic form depicting the past and the present in a meticulous artistic and philosophical manner. Detailed historical analysis serves as a “chain” and always gives a basis for philosophical interpretation.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"4141668451540532304\" href=\"#\"       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/205563.Me_a_Selimovi_">About Meša Selimović</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/205563.Me_a_Selimovi_"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1245971103p3/205563.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/205563.Me_a_Selimovi_">Meša Selimović
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
693
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17756906597919380532">Mehmed "Meša" Selimović was a Yugoslav and a Bosnian writer from Bosnia and Herzegovina and one of the greatest Bosnian writers of the 20th century. His most famous works deal with Bosnia and Herzegovina and the culture of the Bosniak inhabitants of the Ottoman province of Bosnia.</span>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>

a call to SignInPromptController#get to determine if the user should see the sign in interstial.

last seen the insterstitial.  If the controller responds indicating the popup should appear, the
